ترجمة المصنف

وكذلك أحضره درس العالم العلامة الشيخ الكبير، المحدث الشهير، الشيخ أحمد العطار، واستجازه له فأجازه، وكتب له إجازة عامة على ظهر ثبته بخطه مؤرخة في منتصف محرم الحرام سنة ست عشرة ومائتين وألف . قد ترجمه سيدى المرحوم الوالد في ثبته عقود اللآلئ ترجمة حسنة، ورثاه عند وفاته مع غروب الشمس نهار الخميس التاسع من ربيع الثاني سنة ثمان عشرة ومائتين وألف بقصيدة مؤرخا وفاته بها .
وقد أخذ سيدى عن مشايخ كثيرين منهم : الشيخ الأمير الكبير المصرى، وأجازه إجازة عامة كتبها له بخطه الشريف، وختمها بختمه المنيف، وأرسلها له مؤرخة في غرة رمضان المعظم قدره من شهور عام ثمانية وعشرين بعد الألف والمائتين من الهجرة النبوية، وكذا أخذ عن مشايخ كثيرين يطول ذكرهم هنا من شاميين ومصريين، وحجازيين وعراقيين، وروميين. وكان له عم من أهل الصلاح ومظنة الولاية ومن أهل الكشف، اسمه الشيخ صالح ، اسم على مسمى، حتى إنه بشر أمه به قبل ولادته، وهو سمـاه مـحـمـد أمين حين كان في بطن أمه، يضعه في حال صغره في حجره ويقول له : أعطيتك عطية الأسياد في رأسك " .
حياته العلمية :
كان رحمه الله شغله من الدنيا التعلم والتعليم، والتفهم والتفهيم والإقبال على مولاه، والسعى في اكتساب رضاه، مقسما زمنه على أنواع الطاعات، والعبادات والإفادات من صيام وقيام، وتدريس وإفتاء وتأليف على الدوام. وكان له ذوق فى حل مشكلات القوم، وله بهم الاعتقاد العظيم، ويعامله بالاحترام والتكريم .
وكان رحمه الله تعالى جعل وقت التأليف والتحرير في الليل فلا ينام منه إلا ما قل. وجعل النهار للدروس وإفادة التلامذة، وإفادة المستفتين.
Introduction to the Investigation
12
Biography of the Author

Al-Qadiriyya, from his shaykh al-Shaykh Muhammad Shakir, may God have mercy on him, until someone reliable about his righteousness and religion among those who accompanied him in his travel from his students informed me: 'I never found anything that defames him in his worldly life or his religion.' He was of good character and traits; we never heard in my travel with him on the road to Hajj that he spoke a word that angered anyone from his companions and servants, or anyone from all people. Except if he saw an evil, he would change it immediately according to the pure, just Shariah. Questions used to come to him from most regions, and many people benefited from him, both city dwellers and nomads.

My master, may God Almighty have mercy on him, once went with his shaykh al-Sayyid Muhammad Shakir to visit some of the scholars and righteous of India, al-Shaykh Muhammad Abd al-Nabi when he arrived in Damascus. When they entered upon him, my master's shaykh sat and my master remained standing at the threshold in front of his shaykh, carrying his sandals in his hand as was his habit with his shaykh. Then al-Shaykh Muhammad Abd al-Nabi said to my master's shaykh: 'Order this Sayyid youth to sit, for I will not sit until he sits; indeed his hand will be kissed and benefit will be taken from his merit in all regions, and upon him is the light of the family of prophecy.' So al-Shaykh Muhammad Shakir said: 'Sit, my son.' And similarly occurred to him with his mentioned shaykh an indication similar to this from the famous Sufi Imam and great saint al-Shaykh Taha al-Kurdi, may God sanctify his secret. From that time, the shaykh's care for him and attention to him increased in teaching.

His mentioned shaykh often used to take him with him and attend the lessons of his shaykhs, until he took him and attended the lesson of his shaykh, the scholar, the pious, the hadith master al-Shaykh Muhammad al-Kazbari, and asked him to be clearly seen, so he gave him permission (ijaza) and wrote for him a general permission on the back of his register (thabit), dated at the beginning of the night of the first of [month?] ten and two hundred and one thousand? And [unclear] translated him in his register with a biography and also eulogized him, my master, at his death on the night of Friday, the nineteenth night of Rabi' al-Awwal in the year [unclear] and twenty and two hundred and one thousand, with a poem dating his death therein.

[PAGE_013]
Introduction to the Investigation
13
Biography of the Author

And similarly he attended the lesson of the scholar, the great shaykh, the famous muhaddith, al-Shaykh Ahmad al-Attar, and asked him to be granted permission, so he gave him permission and wrote for him a general permission on the back of his register in his own handwriting, dated in the middle of Muharram al-Haram in the year sixteen and two hundred and one thousand. My deceased master, the father, translated him in his register 'Uqud al-La'ali' with a good translation, and eulogized him at his death on Thursday late afternoon, the ninth of Rabi' al-Thani in the year eighteen and two hundred and one thousand, with a poem dating his death.

My master took from many shaykhs, among them: al-Shaykh al-Amir al-Kabir al-Misri, and he gave him a general permission that he wrote in his noble handwriting, and sealed it with his eminent seal, and sent it to him dated on the first of Ramadan, exalted in value, of the months of the year twenty-eight after one thousand two hundred of the Hijra of the Prophet. Similarly, he took from many shaykhs whose mention would be lengthy here, from Syrians, Egyptians, Hijazis, Iraqis, and Romans. He had an uncle from among the righteous and likely saints, and from the people of unveiling, named al-Shaykh Salih, a name befitting its meaning, until he gave his mother glad tidings of him before his birth, and he named him Muhammad Amin while he was in his mother's womb. He would place him, while he was small, in his lap and say to him: 'I have given you the gift of masters on your head.'

His Scholarly Life:
May God have mercy on him, his occupation in this world was learning and teaching, understanding and making understand, turning to his Lord, and striving to earn His pleasure, dividing his time into types of obedience, worship, and benefits: fasting, night prayer, teaching, issuing fatwas, and authoring continuously. He had a taste in solving the problems of the people, and he had great belief in them, and he treated them with respect and honor.

May God Almighty have mercy on him, he set the time for authorship and writing at night, so he would not sleep except a little. And he made the daytime for lessons, benefiting students, and benefiting those seeking fatwas.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the author (Sayyid Muhammad Amin?) focusing on his character, his relationship with his shaykhs, and his scholarly routine. It begins by describing his impeccable character: he was blameless in both worldly and religious matters, never speaking harshly unless to correct an evil. His reputation attracted questions from far and wide, and many benefited from him. The biography then relates a significant incident: during a visit to the Indian scholar Shaykh Muhammad Abd al-Nabi, the author stood in deference to his shaykh, carrying his sandals. The visiting scholar recognized the author's future prominence and insisted he sit, foretelling that his hand would be kissed and his knowledge would spread. This event, along with a similar indication from the Sufi saint Shaykh Taha al-Kurdi, led to increased attention from his shaykh. The author's shaykh would take him to lessons of other renowned scholars, such as Shaykh Muhammad al-Kazbari and Shaykh Ahmad al-Attar, who all granted him general permissions (ijazat 'amma). These permissions were documented on the back of the shaykhs' own registers, indicating the high level of trust and authority conferred upon him. The biography also notes that the author's own father translated and eulogized these shaykhs. The author studied under a wide network of scholars from various regions: Syria, Egypt, Hijaz, Iraq, and Rum (Anatolia). Interestingly, his uncle, a righteous saint, had foretold his birth and named him Muhammad Amin. The passage concludes with a description of his scholarly life: he dedicated his nights to writing and authorship, sleeping very little, and his days to teaching and issuing fatwas, serving both students and the general public.

Key Terms
إجازة عامة (ijāzat ‘āmma): A general permission (license) to transmit and teach all or most of the shaykh's works and knowledge, as opposed to a specific ijazah for a particular book.
ثبته (thabituh): His register or notebook; a collection of his teachers, works, and permissions. Often called 'thabit' or 'thabt' in scholarly circles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A general permission (ijāzat 'āmma)
C) A letter of recommendation
D) A summary of a lesson

Key Takeaway
Formal authorization (ijāzah) from a qualified teacher is essential in the Hanafī tradition for transmitting knowledge and issuing fatwas with authority.

The Character and Scholarly Life of a Mufti
Narrative of the author's character and scholarly routine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the ethical qualities (adab) and daily discipline of a classical Hanafī mufti as exemplified by the author, and apply these qualities to modern mufti conduct.

Core Idea
A mufti's character and routine are integral to his authority and the correctness of his fatwas.

This biographical passage not only lists the author's teachers but also vividly portrays his character: his impeccable manners, his deference to his shaykh, his dedication to teaching and writing, and his avoidance of anger except to correct wrongs. These details are not incidental; they form the adab (etiquette) expected of a mufti. In the Hanafī tradition, the personal piety and discipline of a mufti are considered essential prerequisites for issuing fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Extract the character traits explicitly mentioned
Read the passage and list the qualities attributed to the author, such as his good conduct, his respect for teachers, and his anger only for the sake of Shariah.
Focus on how these traits are demonstrated in specific incidents.
2
Analyze the teacher-student relationship
Look at the incident where the author stands at the threshold carrying his shaykh's sandals. What does this show about the required respect for teachers?
In Hanafi adab, respect for teachers is a prerequisite for effective learning.
3
Examine the daily routine
The passage describes how the author divided his time between day (teaching, fatwa) and night (writing). Explain why this balance is important.
A mufti must manage time wisely to serve the community and continue his own scholarship.
4
Connect to adab al-mufti
Refer to classical works on adab al-mufti (e.g., Ibn Abidin's Rasm al-Mufti) and identify which of these qualities are stressed there.
For example, avoiding anger, humility, and constant learning are key adab.

Biography of the Author

Al-Qadiriyya, from his shaykh al-Shaykh Muhammad Shakir, may God have mercy on him, until someone reliable about his righteousness and religion among those who accompanied him in his travel from his students informed me: 'I never found anything that defames him in his worldly life or his religion.' Except if he saw an evil, he would change it immediately according to the pure, just Shariah. Questions used to come to him from most regions, and many people benefited from him, both city dwellers and nomads.

My master, may God Almighty have mercy on him, once went with his shaykh al-Sayyid Muhammad Shakir to visit some of the scholars and righteous of India, al-Shaykh Muhammad Abd al-Nabi when he arrived in Damascus. When they entered upon him, my master's shaykh sat and my master remained standing at the threshold in front of his shaykh, carrying his sandals in his hand as was his habit with his shaykh. Then al-Shaykh Muhammad Abd al-Nabi said to my master's shaykh: 'Order this Sayyid youth to sit, for I will not sit until he sits; indeed his hand will be kissed and benefit will be taken from his merit in all regions, and upon him is the light of the family of prophecy.' So al-Shaykh Muhammad Shakir said: 'Sit, my son.' And similarly occurred to him with his mentioned shaykh an indication similar to this from the famous Sufi Imam and great saint al-Shaykh Taha al-Kurdi, may God sanctify his secret. From that time, the shaykh's care for him and attention to him increased in teaching.

His mentioned shaykh often used to take him with him and attend the lessons of his shaykhs, until he took him and attended the lesson of his shaykh, the scholar, the pious, the hadith master al-Shaykh Muhammad al-Kazbari, and asked him to be clearly seen, so he gave him permission (ijaza) and wrote for him a general permission on the back of his register (thabit), dated at the beginning of the night of the first of [month?] ten and two hundred and one thousand? And [unclear] translated him in his register with a biography and also eulogized him, my master, at his death on the night of Friday, the nineteenth night of Rabi' al-Awwal in the year [unclear] and twenty and two hundred and one thousand, with a poem dating his death therein.

[PAGE_013]
Introduction to the Investigation
13
Biography of the Author

And similarly he attended the lesson of the scholar, the great shaykh, the famous muhaddith, al-Shaykh Ahmad al-Attar, and asked him to be granted permission, so he gave him permission and wrote for him a general permission on the back of his register in his own handwriting, dated in the middle of Muharram al-Haram in the year sixteen and two hundred and one thousand. My deceased master, the father, translated him in his register 'Uqud al-La'ali' with a good translation, and eulogized him at his death on Thursday late afternoon, the ninth of Rabi' al-Thani in the year eighteen and two hundred and one thousand, with a poem dating his death.

My master took from many shaykhs, among them: al-Shaykh al-Amir al-Kabir al-Misri, and he gave him a general permission that he wrote in his noble handwriting, and sealed it with his eminent seal, and sent it to him dated on the first of Ramadan, exalted in value, of the months of the year twenty-eight after one thousand two hundred of the Hijra of the Prophet. Similarly, he took from many shaykhs whose mention would be lengthy here, from Syrians, Egyptians, Hijazis, Iraqis, and Romans. He had an uncle from among the righteous and likely saints, and from the people of unveiling, named al-Shaykh Salih, a name befitting its meaning, until he gave his mother glad tidings of him before his birth, and he named him Muhammad Amin while he was in his mother's womb. He would place him, while he was small, in his lap and say to him: 'I have given you the gift of masters on your head.'

His Scholarly Life:
May God have mercy on him, his occupation in this world was learning and teaching, understanding and making understand, turning to his Lord, and striving to earn His pleasure, dividing his time into types of obedience, worship, and benefits: fasting, night prayer, teaching, issuing fatwas, and authoring continuously. He had a taste in solving the problems of the people, and he had great belief in them, and he treated them with respect and honor.

May God Almighty have mercy on him, he set the time for authorship and writing at night, so he would not sleep except a little. And he made the daytime for lessons, benefiting students, and benefiting those seeking fatwas.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the author (Sayyid Muhammad Amin?) focusing on his character, his relationship with his shaykhs, and his scholarly routine. It begins by describing his impeccable character: he was blameless in both worldly and religious matters, never speaking harshly unless to correct an evil. His reputation attracted questions from far and wide, and many benefited from him. The biography then relates a significant incident: during a visit to the Indian scholar Shaykh Muhammad Abd al-Nabi, the author stood in deference to his shaykh, carrying his sandals. The visiting scholar recognized the author's future prominence and insisted he sit, foretelling that his hand would be kissed and his knowledge would spread. This event, along with a similar indication from the Sufi saint Shaykh Taha al-Kurdi, led to increased attention from his shaykh. The author's shaykh would take him to lessons of other renowned scholars, such as Shaykh Muhammad al-Kazbari and Shaykh Ahmad al-Attar, who all granted him general permissions (ijazat 'amma). These permissions were documented on the back of the shaykhs' own registers, indicating the high level of trust and authority conferred upon him. The biography also notes that the author's own father translated and eulogized these shaykhs. The author studied under a wide network of scholars from various regions: Syria, Egypt, Hijaz, Iraq, and Rum (Anatolia). Interestingly, his uncle, a righteous saint, had foretold his birth and named him Muhammad Amin. The passage concludes with a description of his scholarly life: he dedicated his nights to writing and authorship, sleeping very little, and his days to teaching and issuing fatwas, serving both students and the general public.

Key Terms
سيدي (sayyidī): My master; a term of respect used by the author (or the commentator) referring to the subject of the biography (the author himself or a revered shaykh). In this text, it refers to the author (the one being translated).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what prompted the author to speak or act against something?
A) Personal offense
B) Seeing an evil (munkar) that needed correction
C) Disagreement with a student
D) To demonstrate his authority

Key Takeaway
A mufti's character and disciplined lifestyle are foundational to his role, as shown by the author's impeccable conduct and balanced routine.

The Scholarly Routine of a Hanafi Mufti: Adab from the Author's Life
Biographical description of the author's scholarly habits and daily routine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of a mufti's daily routine as exemplified by the author and apply them to the concept of adab al-mufti.

Core Idea
A mufti's day balances teaching, fatwa, authorship, and worship with discipline and devotion.

This passage describes the daily life of a great Hanafi scholar and mufti. His habits offer a model for the adab (conduct) expected of a mufti: meticulous scholarship, constant worship, and dedication to serving the community. Let us extract practical lessons for the mufti's craft.

Guided Path
1
Extract daily activities
From the text, list all the activities the author engaged in daily: teaching, fatwa, authorship, fasting, night prayer, recitation, etc.
Don't miss the detail about constant wudu.
2
Categorize activities
Group them into three categories: scholarly (teaching, writing, correcting books), spiritual (prayer, fasting, Quran), and worldly (managing affairs through a partner).
This reveals the holistic nature of a mufti's life.
3
Analyze time allocation
The author explicitly allocated night for authorship and day for teaching and fatwa. Note also his Ramadan practice of completing one khatm per night.
Time management is a key part of adab al-mufti.
4
Identify virtues
Hint at the qualities that made him a reliable mufti: precision in correcting texts, beautiful handwriting, fairness to mustaftis (he often modified questions), and modesty.
These are not just personal traits but part of mufti adab.
5
Derive principles for mufti conduct
From these observations, formulate principles such as: 'A mufti must maintain constant scholarship and spiritual vigilance' and 'A mufti should answer with care and precision.'
Relate these to the classical works on adab al-mufti, e.g., Ibn Abidin's own Rasm al-Mufti.

وكذلك أحضره درس العالم العلامة الشيخ الكبير، المحدث الشهير، الشيخ أحمد العطار، واستجازه له فأجازه، وكتب له إجازة عامة على ظهر ثبته بخطه مؤرخة في منتصف محرم الحرام سنة ست عشرة ومائتين وألف. قد ترجمه سيدى المرحوم الوالد في ثبته عقود اللآلئ ترجمة حسنة، ورثاه عند وفاته مع غروب الشمس نهار الخميس التاسع من ربيع الثاني سنة ثمان عشرة ومائتين وألف بقصيدة مؤرخا وفاته بها. وقد أخذ سيدى عن مشايخ كثيرين منهم: الشيخ الأمير الكبير المصرى، وأجازه إجازة عامة كتبها له بخطه الشريف، وختمها بختمه المنيف، وأرسلها له مؤرخة في غرة رمضان المعظم قدره من شهور عام ثمانية وعشرين بعد الألف والمائتين من الهجرة النبوية، وكذا أخذ عن مشايخ كثيرين يطول ذكرهم هنا من شاميين ومصريين، وحجازيين وعراقيين، وروميين. وكان له عم من أهل الصلاح ومظنة الولاية ومن أهل الكشف، اسمه الشيخ صالح، اسم على مسمى، حتى إنه بشر أمه به قبل ولادته، وهو سماه محمد أمين حين كان في بطن أمه، يضعه في حال صغره في حجره ويقول له: "أعطيتك عطية الأسياد في رأسك". حياته العلمية: كان رحمه الله شغله من الدنيا التعلم والتعليم، والتفهم والتفهيم والإقبال على مولاه، والسعى في اكتساب رضاه، مقسما زمنه على أنواع الطاعات، والعبادات والإفادات من صيام وقيام، وتدريس وإفتاء وتأليف على الدوام. وكان له ذوق فى حل مشكلات القوم، وله بهم الاعتقاد العظيم، ويعامله بالاحترام والتكريم. وكان رحمه الله تعالى جعل وقت التأليف والتحرير في الليل فلا ينام منه إلا ما قل. وجعل النهار للدروس وإفادة التلامذة، وإفادة المستفتين. ويلاحظ أمر دنياه شريكه من غير أن يتعاطى بنفسه. وكان في رمضان يختم كل ليلة ختمة كاملا مع تدبر معانيه، وكثيراً ما يستغرق ليله بالبكاء والقراءة، ولا يدع وقتاً من الأوقات إلا وهو على طهارة، ويثابر الوضوء على الوضوء. وكان رحمه الله تعالى مغرماً بتصحيح الكتب والكتابة عليها، فلا يدع شيئاً من قيد أو اعتراض أو تنبيه أو جواب أو تتمة فائدة إلا ويكتبه على الهامش ويكتب المطالب أيضاً. وكانت عنده كتب من سائر العلوم لم يجمع على منوالها، وكان كثير منها بخط يده، ولم يدع كتاباً منها إلا وعليه كتابته. وكان السبب في جمعه لهذه الكتب العديمة النظير والذه [؟]، فإنه كان يشتري له كل كتاب أراده ويقول له: "اشتر ما بدا لك من الكتب وأنا أدفع لك الثمن، فإنك أحييت ما أمته أنا من سيرة سلفي، فجزاك الله تعالى خيرا يا ولدي" وأعطاه كتب أسلافه الموجودة عنده من أثرهم الموقوفة على ذراريهم، وعندي بعض منها، والله تعالى الحمد. وكان رحمه الله تعالى حريصاً على إصلاح الكتب، لا يمر على موضع منها فيه غلط إلا أصلحه وكتب عليه ما يناسبه، وكان حسن الخط، حسن القشط، قل أن يرى من يكتب مثله على الفتاوى وعلى هوامش الكتب في الجودة وحسن الخط وتناسق الأسطر وتناسبها، ولا يكتب على سؤال رفع إليه إلا أن يغيره غالبا فلذلك كان تعاليقه على هوامش الكتب وخواشيها وكتابته على أسئلة المستفتين والأوراق التي سودها بالمباحث الرائقة، والرقائق الفائقة، فلا يكاد أن تحصى ولا يمكن أن تستقصى. وكان رحمه الله تعالى فقيه النفس انفرد به في زمنه، بحاثاً ما باحثه أحد إلا وظهر عليه. وقد حكى تلميذه صاحب الفضيلة العلامة محمد أفندى جابي زاده قاضي المدينة المنورة: أن شيخ الإسلام عارف عصمت بك مفتى السلطنة بدار الخلافة العلية قال له: إني كنت أؤمل أن تطلب لي الإجازة من
Introduction to the Verification / Biography of the Author / Likewise, he attended the lesson of the great scholar, the famous muhaddith, Shaykh Ahmad al-Attar, and sought his ijaza, and he granted it, and wrote him a general ijaza on the back of his thabat in his own handwriting dated mid-Muharram 1216 AH. My late father translated him in his thabat 'Uqud al-La'ali' with a good biography, and eulogized him upon his death on Thursday 9th Rabi' al-Thani 1218 AH with a poem dating his death. My master took from many shuyukh, among them: Shaykh al-Amir al-Kabir al-Misri, who gave him a general ijaza, wrote it in his noble handwriting, sealed it with his exalted seal, and sent it to him dated 1st Ramadan 1228 AH. He also took from many other shuyukh, too many to mention here, from the Levant, Egypt, Hejaz, Iraq, and the Roman lands. He had an uncle from among the righteous, one presumed to be a saint and possessor of kashf, named Shaykh Salih, a name true to its meaning, as he gave his mother glad tidings of him before his birth, and he named him Muhammad Amin while he was in his mother's womb; he would place him in his lap as a child and say: 'I have given you the gift of the masters in your head.' His academic life: May Allah have mercy on him, his occupation in this world was learning and teaching, understanding and making understand, turning to his Lord, and striving to earn His pleasure, dividing his time among types of obedience, worship, and benefits: fasting, night prayer, teaching, giving fatwa, and authorship perpetually. He had a taste for solving the people's problems, had great belief in them, and treated them with respect and honor. He set the time for authorship and writing at night, sleeping little. He made the day for lessons, benefiting students, and benefiting mustaftis. He supervised his worldly affairs through a partner without personally engaging in them. In Ramadan, he would complete a full khatm each night with contemplation of its meanings, and often spent his night crying and reading, and he never left any time except that he was in a state of purity, and he would renew wudu upon wudu. He was obsessed with correcting books and writing on them, leaving no restriction, objection, note, answer, or completion of benefit without writing it in the margin, and he would also write topics. He had books from various sciences unmatched in their kind, many in his own handwriting, and he left no book without his writing on it. The reason for his collection of these rare and valuable books was that his father would buy for him every book he wanted and say: 'Buy whatever books you desire, and I will pay the price, for you have revived what I have killed of the way of my predecessors, may Allah reward you well, my son.' And he gave him the books of his ancestors that were with him as endowments for their descendants, and I have some of them, praise be to Allah. He was keen on correcting books, not passing a place with an error without correcting it and writing what was appropriate. He had good handwriting and good erasure; rarely was someone seen who wrote like him on fatwas and on book margins in quality, beauty of handwriting, and regularity of lines. He would not write an answer to a question submitted to him without modifying it generally, so his marginalia on books and his writing on the questions of mustaftis and the papers he darkened with excellent investigations and fine subtleties are almost uncountable and cannot be fully surveyed. He was a jurist by nature, unique in his time; he was a researcher whom no one could debate without his victory. His student, the virtuous scholar Muhammad Afandi Jabi Zadah, qadi of Medina, narrated: That Shaykh al-Islam 'Arif 'Ismat Bek, mufti of the Sultanate in the Sublime Dar al-Khilafah, said to him: 'I was hoping that you would request an ijaza for me from...'
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the author (likely Muhammad Amin ibn Abidin). It describes his education, teachers, scholarly habits, and character. He attended the lessons of Shaykh Ahmad al-Attar and received a general ijaza. His own father (referred to as 'sidi' and 'the late parent') wrote a biography of him and eulogized him. He studied under many scholars from various regions. His uncle, Shaykh Salih, was a righteous man who named him Muhammad Amin. The author's daily life was divided between teaching during the day and authorship at night. He was meticulous about correcting books, writing marginalia, and maintaining purity. His father encouraged his love of book collecting. He was a skilled calligrapher and would modify fatwa questions before answering. He was a peerless jurist and researcher. The passage ends with a story from his student about Shaykh al-Islam 'Arif 'Ismat Bek requesting an ijaza, though the text cuts off.

Key Terms
إجازة عامة (ijāzah ʿāmmah): A general permission to transmit and teach the works of the granting scholar, without specifying particular books.
ثبت (thabat): A biographical register or list of a scholar's teachers and the works he studied, often containing ijazat.
مفتى السلطنة (muftī al-salṭanah): The highest-ranking mufti of the Ottoman state, also known as Shaykh al-Islam.
مستفتي (mustaftī): A person who seeks a fatwa (questioner).

Key Terms from Source Passage

The author's daily routine demonstrates that a mufti must integrate teaching, fatwa, authorship, and spiritual devotion with meticulous discipline.

The Role of Ijaza and Thabat in Hanafi Knowledge Transmission
Biographical description highlighting the author's acquisition of ijazat and mention of his father's thabat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the significance of ijaza and thabat in the Hanafi tradition and describe how they establish scholarly authority.

Core Idea
Ijaza and thabat are the backbone of Hanafi transmission, linking the mufti to authoritative chains.

The passage begins with the author seeking and receiving ijazat from great scholars, and mentions his father's thabat. These terms are central to how Hanafi knowledge is authenticated. This lesson unpacks the concepts of ijaza and thabat, showing how they function both in the author's life and in the broader tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the occurrences of ijaza in the text
Find the two specific grants of ijaza mentioned: from Shaykh Ahmad al-Attar and from Shaykh al-Amir al-Kabir al-Misri. Note the details: dates, location, and type (general ijaza).
Also note that the author's father's thabat contains a biography and eulogy.
2
Define ijaza and thabat using the text
From the descriptions, deduce what an ijaza is (a permission to transmit) and what a thabat is (a register of teachers and books). The phrase 'general ijaza' indicates a broad permission.
Cross-reference with the key terms.
3
Understand the purpose of recording ijazat
The text mentions that the ijaza was written on the back of the thabat, and the thabat included a biography. This shows that ijazat are documented to preserve the chain of transmission.
This is part of the Hanafi emphasis on sanad.
4
Connect ijaza to mufti authority
The author's student later sought an ijaza for the Shaykh al-Islam, indicating that even high-ranking muftis value formal authorization. The ijaza authenticates the mufti's competence and links him to the scholarly lineage.
This elevates the mufti's fatwa from personal opinion to transmitted authority.
5
Reflect on the value of thabat as academic lineage
The author's father's thabat is described as 'Uqud al-La'ali', suggesting a carefully curated record. Thabats function like academic genealogies, protecting the integrity of knowledge.
Think of it as a 'passport' for scholarship.

بخط يده، ولم يدع كتاباً منها إلا وعليه كتابته. وكان السبب في جمعه لهذه الكتب العديمة النظير والذه [؟]، فإنه كان يشتري له كل كتاب أراده ويقول له: "اشتر ما بدا لك من الكتب وأنا أدفع لك الثمن، فإنك أحييت ما أمته أنا من سيرة سلفي، فجزاك الله تعالى خيرا يا ولدي" وأعطاه كتب أسلافه الموجودة عنده من أثرهم الموقوفة على ذراريهم، وعندي بعض منها، والله تعالى الحمد. وكان رحمه الله تعالى حريصاً على إصلاح الكتب، لا يمر على موضع منها فيه غلط إلا أصلحه وكتب عليه ما يناسبه، وكان حسن الخط، حسن القشط، قل أن يرى من يكتب مثله على الفتاوى وعلى هوامش الكتب في الجودة وحسن الخط وتناسق الأسطر وتناسبها، ولا يكتب على سؤال رفع إليه إلا أن يغيره غالبا فلذلك كان تعاليقه على هوامش الكتب وخواشيها وكتابته على أسئلة المستفتين والأوراق التي سودها بالمباحث الرائقة، والرقائق الفائقة، فلا يكاد أن تحصى ولا يمكن أن تستقصى. وكان رحمه الله تعالى فقيه النفس انفرد به في زمنه، بحاثاً ما باحثه أحد إلا وظهر عليه. وقد حكى تلميذه صاحب الفضيلة العلامة محمد أفندى جابي زاده قاضي المدينة المنورة: أن شيخ الإسلام عارف عصمت بك مفتى السلطنة بدار الخلافة العلية قال له: إني كنت أؤمل أن تطلب لي الإجازة من
Introduction to the Verification / Biography of the Author / Likewise, he attended the lesson of the great scholar, the famous muhaddith, Shaykh Ahmad al-Attar, and sought his ijaza, and he granted it, and wrote him a general ijaza on the back of his thabat in his own handwriting dated mid-Muharram 1216 AH. My late father translated him in his thabat 'Uqud al-La'ali' with a good biography, and eulogized him upon his death on Thursday 9th Rabi' al-Thani 1218 AH with a poem dating his death. My master took from many shuyukh, among them: Shaykh al-Amir al-Kabir al-Misri, who gave him a general ijaza, wrote it in his noble handwriting, sealed it with his exalted seal, and sent it to him dated 1st Ramadan 1228 AH. He also took from many other shuyukh, too many to mention here, from the Levant, Egypt, Hejaz, Iraq, and the Roman lands. He had an uncle from among the righteous, one presumed to be a saint and possessor of kashf, named Shaykh Salih, a name true to its meaning, as he gave his mother glad tidings of him before his birth, and he named him Muhammad Amin while he was in his mother's womb; he would place him in his lap as a child and say: 'I have given you the gift of the masters in your head.' His academic life: May Allah have mercy on him, his occupation in this world was learning and teaching, understanding and making understand, turning to his Lord, and striving to earn His pleasure, dividing his time among types of obedience, worship, and benefits: fasting, night prayer, teaching, giving fatwa, and authorship perpetually. He had a taste for solving the people's problems, had great belief in them, and treated them with respect and honor. He set the time for authorship and writing at night, sleeping little. He made the day for lessons, benefiting students, and benefiting mustaftis. He supervised his worldly affairs through a partner without personally engaging in them. In Ramadan, he would complete a full khatm each night with contemplation of its meanings, and often spent his night crying and reading, and he never left any time except that he was in a state of purity, and he would renew wudu upon wudu. He was obsessed with correcting books and writing on them, leaving no restriction, objection, note, answer, or completion of benefit without writing it in the margin, and he would also write topics. He had books from various sciences unmatched in their kind, many in his own handwriting, and he left no book without his writing on it. The reason for his collection of these rare and valuable books was that his father would buy for him every book he wanted and say: 'Buy whatever books you desire, and I will pay the price, for you have revived what I have killed of the way of my predecessors, may Allah reward you well, my son.' And he gave him the books of his ancestors that were with him as endowments for their descendants, and I have some of them, praise be to Allah. He was keen on correcting books, not passing a place with an error without correcting it and writing what was appropriate. He had good handwriting and good erasure; rarely was someone seen who wrote like him on fatwas and on book margins in quality, beauty of handwriting, and regularity of lines. He would not write an answer to a question submitted to him without modifying it generally, so his marginalia on books and his writing on the questions of mustaftis and the papers he darkened with excellent investigations and fine subtleties are almost uncountable and cannot be fully surveyed. He was a jurist by nature, unique in his time; he was a researcher whom no one could debate without his victory. His student, the virtuous scholar Muhammad Afandi Jabi Zadah, qadi of Medina, narrated: That Shaykh al-Islam 'Arif 'Ismat Bek, mufti of the Sultanate in the Sublime Dar al-Khilafah, said to him: 'I was hoping that you would request an ijaza for me from...'
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the author (likely Muhammad Amin ibn Abidin). It describes his education, teachers, scholarly habits, and character. He attended the lessons of Shaykh Ahmad al-Attar and received a general ijaza. His own father (referred to as 'sidi' and 'the late parent') wrote a biography of him and eulogized him. He studied under many scholars from various regions. His uncle, Shaykh Salih, was a righteous man who named him Muhammad Amin. The author's daily life was divided between teaching during the day and authorship at night. He was meticulous about correcting books, writing marginalia, and maintaining purity. His father encouraged his love of book collecting. He was a skilled calligrapher and would modify fatwa questions before answering. He was a peerless jurist and researcher. The passage ends with a story from his student about Shaykh al-Islam 'Arif 'Ismat Bek requesting an ijaza, though the text cuts off.

Key Terms
إجازة عامة (ijāzah ʿāmmah): A general permission to transmit and teach the works of the granting scholar, without specifying particular books.
ثبت (thabat): A biographical register or list of a scholar's teachers and the works he studied, often containing ijazat.
مفتى السلطنة (muftī al-salṭanah): The highest-ranking mufti of the Ottoman state, also known as Shaykh al-Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only for family records without scholarly purpose.
B) To preserve the memory and chain of the author, showing the importance of personal bonds in transmission.
C) To fulfill a legal requirement for fatwa.
D) To ensure the author's books were inherited properly.

Key Takeaway
Ijaza and thabat are essential tools in the Hanafi tradition for authenticating scholarly authority and preserving the chain of knowledge.

The Scholarly Pedigree of a Hanafi Mufti: Biography of the Author of Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti
Describes the author's habits, book collecting, teaching, and licenses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a Hanafi mufti's personal piety, teaching skill, and chain of ijazat are integral to his authority in issuing fatwas.

Core Idea
A mufti's authority rests on personal piety, scholarly mastery, and an unbroken golden chain of transmission.

This biographical passage introduces us to the author of a key text on the methodology of ifta (Rasm al-Mufti). By examining his daily habits, his devotion to books, his teaching style, and the scholarly licenses he received, we see how Hanafi tradition constructs the ideal mufti. This lesson connects these personal details to the larger framework of mufti adab (ethics) and the hierarchy of fatwa authority.

Guided Path
1
Observe the author's personal devotion
The text describes his Quran recitation with contemplation, constant wudu, and weeping during worship. Why does a mufti need spiritual discipline? Hanafi adab emphasizes taqwa as a prerequisite for ifta.
2
Analyze his relationship with books
He corrected manuscripts, wrote marginalia, and collected a vast library. This tells us that intellectual rigor and meticulousness are essential for a mufti.
3
Evaluate the teaching praise
Students describe his unique ability to clarify and expand. What does this say about the importance of transmitting knowledge accurately?
4
Catalog the ijazat
List the scholarly licenses he obtained, noting the ages and the status of the issuing scholars. How does this establish his authority?
5
Connect to Rasm al-Mufti
This biography is a frontispiece to a work on mufti methodology. Why would the author include his own credentials? It establishes the work's authority.

إجازة عامة من العلامة الشهير الشمس المير، الشيخ محمد الكزبري الكبير، محدث عصره والمتوفى سنة ١٢٢١هـ . كتبها له سنة ١٢١٠هـ، وله من العمر اثنا عشر ربيعاً

ومنها : إجازة عامة أيضًا من العلامة الشهير، الشيخ الأمير الكبير المتوفى سنة ١٢٣٢هـ . أرسلها له سنة ١٢٢٨هـ.
ومنها : إجازة بما تجوز روايته من شيخ قراء دمشق، أول شيوخ المترجم
Introduction to the Investigation
Biography of the Author
And he observed the matter of his world as a partner without engaging in it himself. And during Ramadan he would complete a full recitation of the Quran every night with contemplation of its meanings, and often his night would be absorbed in weeping and recitation, and he would not leave any time except that he was in a state of purity, and he would persist in ablution after ablution.
And he (may Allah have mercy on him) was fond of correcting books and writing upon them. He would not leave any restriction, objection, note, answer, or completion of a benefit except that he would write it on the margin, and he would also write the desired points. He had books from all sciences that were unmatched in their arrangement, and many of them were in his own handwriting. He did not leave any book except that he had written upon it. The reason for his collection of these peerless and precious books was that his father would buy for him every book he wanted and say to him: "Buy whatever books you desire and I will pay the price for you, for you have revived what I have caused to die from the way of my predecessors. May Allah reward you well, my son." And he gave him the books of his predecessors that were in his possession from their legacy, endowed to their descendants. I have some of them with me. Praise be to Allah.
·
And he (may Allah have mercy on him) was keen on correcting books. He would not pass by a place in them containing an error except that he would correct it and write what was appropriate. He had beautiful handwriting and beautiful erasure [quality]. Rarely could one be seen who wrote like him on fatwas and on the margins of books in terms of quality, beautiful handwriting, and the regularity and proportion of lines. He would not write a response to a question raised to him except that he would change it often. Therefore, his comments on the margins and edges of books and his writing on the questions of mustaftis and the papers that he blackened with exquisite research and superior subtleties are almost uncountable and cannot be fully enumerated.
And he (may Allah have mercy on him) was a jurist by nature, unique in his time. He was a researcher; no one debated him except that he prevailed over him. His student, the virtuous scholar Muhammad Afandi Jabi Zada, judge of Medina, narrated that Shaykh al-Islam Arif Ismat Bek, the Mufti of the Sultanate in the Sublime Caliphate, said to him: "I had hoped that you would request a license from your teacher for blessing." And his student, the scholar Shaykh Muhammad Afandi Akhwani, Mufti of Beirut, said to me: "I have not heard anything like the exposition of your father, Sayyidi, in his lesson. So much so that I often strive in studying the lesson and review all the glosses, commentaries, and writings on the lesson. I think to myself that I have understood all the difficulties and their answers. Then when I attend the lesson, our teacher explains the lesson and discusses everything I had studied with clarification and comprehension, increasing us with benefits that we had neither heard nor seen. And it never occurred to anyone's mind to mention them."
His Teachers and His Scholarly Licenses:
He obtained many valuable and high scholarly licenses from the eminent scholars of his time among his teachers and the teachers of his teachers. The course of that is that he adhered to his teacher al-Imad in the second decade of his life and began to accompany him to the gatherings of [؟] because he saw in him excellence, intelligence, talent, and acumen. And he would request licenses for him and they would record their lessons for him. Thus, he obtained from that an isnad with a golden chain. Among these licenses were:
A general license from the famous scholar Shams al-Mir, Shaykh Muhammad al-Kuzbari al-Kabir, the muhaddith of his age, who died in 1221 AH. He wrote it for him in 1210 AH, when he was twelve years old.
And among them: a general license also from the great scholar and famous muhaddith, Shaykh Ahmad al-Attar, who died in 1218 AH. He authorized him with it in 1216 AH, when he was eighteen years old.
And among them: a general license also from the famous scholar, Shaykh al-Amir al-Kabir, who died in 1232 AH. He sent it to him in 1228 AH.
And among them: a license for what he may narrate from the shaykh of the reciters of Damascus, the first of the teachers of the author.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a biographical sketch of the author of Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti. It begins by describing his personal devotion: he was mindful of worldly affairs but not personally engaged, he recited the Quran with contemplation during Ramadan, often spent nights in weeping and recitation, and maintained constant ritual purity. He was meticulous with books—correcting them, writing annotations on margins, and collecting a vast library from all sciences. His father supported this passion financially and provided him with legacy books from ancestors.

The author was also skilled in writing fatwas and marginalia, and his annotations were so numerous as to be uncountable. He was a natural jurist and researcher, prevailing in debates. His teaching was highly praised by his students: one student, Muhammad Afandi Jabi Zada, narrated that the Mufti of the Sultanate wished to obtain an ijaza from the author for blessing. Another student, Muhammad Afandi Akhwani, described the author's lessons as uniquely clear and insightful, providing benefits not found elsewhere.

The passage then lists his scholarly licenses (ijazat). He obtained many valuable ijazas from prominent scholars of his time. The first is a general ijaza from Muhammad al-Kuzbari al-Kabir (d. 1221 AH) in 1210 AH when the author was twelve. The second is from Ahmad al-Attar (d. 1218 AH) in 1216 AH at age eighteen. The third is from al-Amir al-Kabir (d. 1232 AH) in 1228 AH. The fourth is a narration license from the shaykh of Quran reciters of Damascus, who is noted as the first of his teachers. This biography establishes the scholarly pedigree and authority of the author, which is crucial for understanding the weight of his work on the methodology of ifta.

Key Terms
إجازة عامة (ijāza 'āmmah): A general license authorizing the recipient to transmit all of the issuing scholar's works and teachings.
سلسلة ذهبية (silsila dhahabiyya): A chain of transmission consisting of highly authoritative scholars, each renowned for their knowledge and piety.
مستفتي (mustaftī): A person who seeks a legal ruling (fatwa) from a mufti.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti's authority is built on personal piety, scholarly meticulousness, and a verified chain of ijazat from renowned teachers.

The Author's Scholarly Network: Ijazat and Lineage in Hanafi Legal Education
Biographical introduction: author's credentials through teachers, ijazat, and students

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of a Hanafi scholar's biography (teachers, ijazat, students) and explain how such a pedigree establishes authority for transmitting works on ifta methodology.

Core Idea
A scholar's ijazat and student lineage form the golden chain that authenticates their teaching.

This lesson introduces you to the author of Sharh Uqood Rasm al-Mufti through the lens of his scholarly network. Understanding who taught him, who licensed him, and who learned from him is essential for appreciating the authority of his work. In the Hanafi tradition, a text's reliability is tied to the chain of transmission (sanad), and this biography provides that chain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the biography
Recognize that this section establishes the author's credentials by listing teachers, ijazat, and students. This is a standard way to validate a scholar's authority in the Islamic tradition.
The biography is not just a personal history; it functions as a chain of transmission (sanad) for the knowledge contained in the book.
2
Note the commendation from a student
Read the opening paragraph where a student praises the author's teaching. This serves as an endorsement of the author's pedagogical skill and depth of knowledge.
Such testimonials are common in biographical entries to add credibility.
3
List the teachers and their ijazat
Extract the names of the author's teachers and the details of the ijazat he received. Pay attention to the age at which he received his first ijazah, as it shows early promise.
His first ijazah at age 12 from al-Kuzbari highlights his precocity and the recognition of his teachers.
4
Analyze the chain of ijazat
Understand that each ijazah is a link in a chain reaching back to earlier authorities. The mention of 'golden series' emphasizes the purity and strength of the transmission.
In Hanafi usul, the strength of a riwayah often depends on the reliability of its sanad.
5
Identify the students and their significance
List the prominent students who studied under the author. Note that some became major scholars themselves, such as al-Maydani (commentator of al-Quduri). This indicates the author's influence in shaping Hanafi scholarship.
A scholar's students are a measure of his impact; many of these students went on to hold important positions like Mufti of Damascus.

ومنها : إجازة عامة أيضاً من العلامة الكبير، والمحدث الشهير، الشيخ أحمد العطار ، المتوفى سنة ۱۲۱٨هـ . أجازه بها سنة ١٢١٦هـ ، وله من العمر ثمانية عشر ربيعاً .
ومنها : إجازة عامة أيضًا من العلامة الشهير، الشيخ الأمير الكبير المتوفى سنة ١٢٣٢هـ . أرسلها له سنة ١٢٢٨هـ.
ومنها : إجازة بما تجوز روايته من شيخ قراء دمشق، أول شيوخ المترجم الشيخ محمد سعيد الحموي، المتوفى سنة ١٢٣٦هـ ، والمجاز من أكابر علماء عصره .
ومنها : إجازة من أجل شيوخه العلامة المسند، الشيخ محمد شاكر العقاد الشهير بـ [اس مقدم سعد؟]
تلامذته :
ممن قرأ عليه وأخذ عنه شقيقه العلامة ، الفاصل، الفقيه الصوفي السيد عبد الغني رحمه الله. ومنهم ولد أخيه المذكور الشيخ أحمد أفنى . أمين الفتوى بدمشق ،حالا صاحب التأليف الشهيرة. ومنهم ابن ابن عمه الشيخ صالح بن السيد حسن عابدين ، ومنهم صاحب الفضيلة والسماحة العالم العلامة عمادة الموانى العظام جابی زاده السيد محمد آفندی، قاضی المدينة المنورة سابقا . ومن أصحاب بايه إسلامبول الحائز للنشيان [؟] العالى المجيدى من الرتبة الثانية من تشرفت في حضرته بأيه إسلامبول، وافتخرت فيه على من نالها بفضائله وعمله الذي أقرته الفحولة وبكمال علومه و قدره مع فضله زاد فيه رفعة وعز النسيان العالى المجيدي من الرتبة الثانية التي فتخرت فيها أعاظم الرجال، وهى فيه فاقت وتبخترت على أكابر أهل الكمال . فإنه أخذ عنه سائر العلوم وبه انتفع ومنهم العالم، العلامة الزاهد العابد الورع، التقى النقي، فقيه النفس، الشيخ يحيى السردست أحد أفاضل الصوفية في زمنه فإنه عنه أخذ وبه انتفع وعـلـيـه تخرج. ومنهم العالم العلامة العمدة الفهامة فقيه العصر، الشيخ عبدالغني الغنيمي الميداني، شارح القدوري وعقيدة الطحاوى، فإنه عنه أخذ وبه انتفع وعليه تخرج. ومنهم العالم العلامة، والعمدة الفهامة، الشيخ حسن البيطار فإنه قرأ عليه العقود الدرية، وعليه تخرج في مذهب السادة الحنفية. ومنهم . ولد المرقوم العالم العلامة الشيخ محمد أفندى البيطار فإنه عنه أخذ وبه
Foreword to the Investigation: Biography of the Author. Your shaykh for blessing, and his student the distinguished scholar Sheikh Muhammad Afandi al-Ikhwani, Mufti of Beirut, said to me: I have not heard anything like the exposition of my lord your father in his lesson. Indeed, I often strive in studying the lesson, and I review all the glosses, commentaries, and writings on the lesson, and I think to myself that I have understood all the problems and their answers. But when I attend the lesson, our shaykh explains the lesson and discusses everything I studied with clarification and explanation, and adds benefits that we have not heard nor seen, nor did it occur to anyone's mind. His teachers and his scholarly licenses: He obtained many valuable and high scholarly licenses from the prominent scholars of his time among his teachers and the teachers of his teachers. The reason for that is that he adhered to his shaykh al-Imad in the second decade of his life, and he used to accompany him to the gatherings of his shaykh? because he saw in him intelligence, cleverness, and acumen, and he would request licenses for him and record their lessons. So he obtained from that a chain with a golden series. Among these licenses: A general license from the famous scholar al-Shams al-Mir, Sheikh Muhammad al-Kuzbari al-Kabir, the hadith scholar of his era, died 1221 AH. He wrote it for him in 1210 AH, when he was twelve springs old. Among them: Also a general license from the great scholar and famous hadith scholar Sheikh Ahmad al-'Attar, died 1218 AH. He licensed him in 1216 AH, when he was eighteen springs old. Among them: Also a general license from the famous scholar Sheikh al-Amir al-Kabir, died 1232 AH. He sent it to him in 1228 AH. Among them: A license for what it is permissible to narrate from the shaykh of Qur'an readers of Damascus, the first of his teachers, Sheikh Muhammad Sa'id al-Hamwi, died 1236 AH, who was licensed by the great scholars of his time. Among them: A license from the most eminent of his teachers, the scholar and musnid Sheikh Muhammad Shakir al-'Aqqad, known as [Is Muqaddam Sa'd?]. His students: Among those who read to him and took from him: his brother, the scholar, the judge, the Sufi jurist al-Sayyid 'Abd al-Ghani, may Allah have mercy on him. Among them: the son of his aforementioned brother, Sheikh Ahmad Afandi, the Amin al-Fatwa in Damascus, currently the author of famous works. Among them: the son of his uncle, Sheikh Salih ibn al-Sayyid Hasan 'Abidin. Among them: the possessor of virtue and tolerance, the scholar, the great one, the dean of great muftis, Jabizada al-Sayyid Muhammad Afandi, former judge of Medina. And from the people of his door in Istanbul, the one who obtained the high Majidi order of the second rank, whom I was honored to be in his presence in Istanbul, and I took pride in him over those who attained it through his virtues and his work which the great scholars approved, and with the perfection of his knowledge and his status along with his virtue, it added to him elevation and honor. The high Majidi order of the second rank, which great men pride themselves in, and in him it excelled and boasted over the great people of perfection. For he took from him all sciences and benefited from him. Among them: the scholar, the great one, the ascetic, the worshiper, the pious, the pure, the insightful jurist, Sheikh Yahya al-Sardast, one of the leading Sufis of his time, for he took from him, benefited from him, and graduated under him. Among them: the scholar, the great one, the master of understanding, the jurist of the age, Sheikh 'Abd al-Ghani al-Ghanimi al-Maydani, commentator of al-Quduri and al-'Aqida al-Tahawiyya, for he took from him, benefited from him, and graduated under him. Among them: the scholar, the great one, the master of understanding, Sheikh Hasan al-Bitar, for he read to him al-'Uqud al-Durriyya, and graduated under him in the Hanafi school. Among them: the son of the aforementioned, the scholar, the great one, Sheikh Muhammad Afandi al-Bitar, for he took from him and benefited from him.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a biographical introduction to the author of Sharh Uqood Rasm al-Mufti. It begins with a commendation by a student of the author, Shaykh Muhammad Afandi al-Ikhwani, who praises the author's father's teaching method. Then it details the author's teachers and the scholarly licenses (ijazat) he received from prominent scholars of his time, including his first ijazah at age 12 from Shaykh Muhammad al-Kuzbari. It also lists his distinguished students, such as Shaykh 'Abd al-Ghani al-Ghanimi al-Maydani, the commentator of al-Quduri, and others. The passage emphasizes the author's high scholarly credentials and his central role in transmitting Hanafi knowledge.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijāzah): A formal license from a scholar authorizing the recipient to transmit or teach certain texts or subjects.
شيخ (shaykh): A teacher or spiritual guide, often with authority in Islamic sciences.

Biography of Shaykh Muhammad Sa'id al-Hamawi
Biographical introduction listing the author's students and works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author of the text, state his death year, and explain his significance in the Hanafi scholarly network through his students and works.

Core Idea
Shaykh Muhammad Sa'id al-Hamawi (d. 1236 AH) was a pivotal Hanafi scholar whose students included many leading jurists and muftis across the Ottoman Empire.

This lesson introduces you to the biography of the author of the work you are studying, Shaykh Muhammad Sa'id al-Hamawi. Understanding his scholarly lineage and the wide influence of his teaching helps establish the authority of the text within the Hanafi tradition. The passage lists his foremost teacher, numerous students who became prominent scholars and judges, and hints at his own works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and his death date
Read the opening line to find the name and death year of the author. This establishes the historical context.
The text says 'المتوفى سنة ١٢٣٦هـ' — 1236 AH.
2
Note his highest teacher and ijaza
The text mentions an ijaza from his chief teacher, Shaykh Muhammad Shakir al-'Aqqad. Recognize that such licenses were crucial for transmitting authority.
Ijazas connect the scholar to earlier generations.
3
Recognize the range of his students
Scan the list of students and their positions (mufti, qadi, commentator). Notice the geographic spread: Damascus, Medina, Istanbul, Egypt, etc.
This shows his influence across the Ottoman Empire.
4
Connect students to key Hanafi works
Identify students who authored significant Hanafi commentaries, such as al-Maydani (al-Quduri) and al-Khalasi (al-Durr al-Mukhtar). This links al-Hamawi to the core curriculum.
These works are pillars of later Hanafi study.
5
Understand the mention of Ibn 'Abidin
The passage ends by mentioning Ibn 'Abidin beginning authorship at seventeen. Clarify that the author is al-Hamawi, not Ibn 'Abidin, but the reference shows continuity of scholarship.
Avoid confusing the two figures.

Commentary
This passage is a biographical introduction to the author of the work, Shaykh Muhammad Sa'id al-Hamawi (d. 1236 AH). It begins by stating his death year and that he was licensed (mujaz) by the leading scholars of his time, particularly from his chief teacher, Shaykh Muhammad Shakir al-'Aqqad. The bulk of the passage lists an extensive number of students who studied under him, graduated from him, and benefited from his knowledge. The list includes many notable Hanafi scholars, such as 'Abd al-Ghani al-Ghanimi al-Maydani (commentator on al-Quduri and al-Tahawi), Hasan al-Baytar, Ahmad al-Islamabali (marginalist on al-Durar), Yusuf Badr al-Din al-Maghribi, 'Abd al-Qadir al-Khalasi (commentator on al-Durr al-Mukhtar), and many others holding positions as muftis, qadis, and scholars across the Ottoman territories. The passage emphasizes the wide reach of the author's scholarship and his high rank in the Hanafi school. The final line begins a section on the author's works, stating that Ibn 'Abidin (likely a reference to the famous Hanafi jurist) started writing at age seventeen, but the text cuts off. This suggests that the author is distinct from Ibn 'Abidin, perhaps a commentator on his work, or the edition is conflating their biographies.

Key Terms
ترجمة المصنف (tarjamat al-musannif): Biography of the author of the work being verified/published.
إجازة (ijaza): A license to transmit knowledge or teach, given by a scholar to a student.
أمين الفتوى (amin al-fatwa): The official mufti or fatwa secretary in a city, responsible for issuing legal opinions.

Life of the Author
beginner
6 min
Understanding Ibn Abidin and His Role in Hanafi Fatwa
Biographical sketch of Ibn Abidin, his students, and his works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the significance of Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar in the Hanafi fatwa tradition and identify his methodology in completing it.

Core Idea
Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar is the cornerstone of Hanafi fatwa, completed by starting where others stopped.

This biographical passage introduces the man behind the most relied-upon reference in the Hanafi school: Muhammad Amin Ibn Abidin. His life and work exemplify how later scholars built upon their predecessors, ensuring the continuity and authority of the madhhab. In this lesson, we will trace his scholarly lineage, understand his motivation for writing, and see how his magnum opus, Radd al-Muhtar, became the go-to text for muftis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and context
Recognize Ibn Abidin as a 19th-century Hanafi jurist who became the Mufti of Damascus. Note the biographical nature of the passage: it lists his teachers, students, and works.
This is a tarjama (biography) from a modern edition of Radd al-Muhtar.
2
Recognize the scholarly network
Scan the long list of students and associates. They include judges, muftis, and scholars from various regions, showing Ibn Abidin's influence.
Names like Ali al-Muradi (Mufti of Damascus) and Abd al-Halim Mulla (qadi askar) show his high-level connections.
3
Understand the motivation for the Hashiya
Ibn Abidin saw that al-Durr al-Mukhtar was too concise and that many earlier commentaries (like Fath al-Qadir) stopped at the chapter of Ijara due to the author's death. He decided to start his commentary from that chapter to complete the unfinished works.
This strategic decision highlights his practical approach to fulfilling a gap in the madhhab literature.
4
Follow the writing method
He began from the chapter of Ijara, then returned to the beginning and wrote up to Ijara again, completing the whole book. He then started making a fair copy but did not finish it in his lifetime.
He worked under the supervision of his teacher Shaykh Sa'id al-Halabi, who famously asked: 'Is it not time for this pile to end?'
5
Note the role of his son
Ibn Abidin's son, Ala al-Din, completed the fair copy after his death and published it as a two-volume supplement titled Qurrat 'Uyun al-Akhyar bi-Takmilat Radd al-Muhtar.
This supplement is essential for understanding the complete text of Radd al-Muhtar.

Introduction to the Investigation 17. Biography of the Author. He benefited [others] and under him they graduated, and he was the Mufti of Damascus at the time. Among them [his students]: the great scholar Ahmad Afandi al-Islamabuli, commentator of al-Durar; he took from him, benefited, and graduated under him. And among them: the virtuous and perfect scholar, the farid of Damascus and chief of its accountants, Sayyid Husayn al-Rassama; he took from him, benefited, and graduated under him. And among them: the great and exemplary scholar, the possessor of beneficial treatises and precious works in the rational and transmitted sciences, Shaykh Yusuf Badr al-Din al-Maghribi; he took from him, benefited, and graduated under him. And among them: the virtuous scholar Shaykh Abd al-Qadir al-Jabi. And among them: Shaykh Muhammad al-Jaqali. And among them: Shaykh Muhammad Afandi al-Munir, one of the companions of the rank of Izmir al-Mujarrada. And among them: the virtuous scholar Shaykh Abd al-Qadir al-Khalasi, commentator of al-Durr al-Mukhtar, al-Alfiyya of Ibn Malik, and others. And among them: the support of noble scholars, Ali Afandi al-Muradi, Mufti of Damascus. And among them: the great and exemplary scholar, the elite of noble scholars, Abd al-Halim Mulla, judge of Damascus and judge of the army of Anatolia. And among them: Shaykh Hasan ibn Khalid Beg. And among them: Shaykh Muhammad Tallu. And among them: Shaykh Muhyi al-Din al-Yafi. And among them: Shaykh Ahmad al-Mahlawi al-Misri, the chief of reciters of his time. And among them: Shaykh Abd al-Rahman al-Jamal al-Misri. And among them: Shaykh Ayyub al-Misri. And among them: Shaykh Mulla Abd al-Razzaq al-Baghdadi, one of the famous and excellent scholars of Baghdad. And among them: Shaykh Muslih, judge of Jenin. And among them: Shaykh Ahmad al-Bizri, judge of Sidon. And among them: his brother Shaykh Muhammad Afandi, its mufti. And among them: Shaykh Muhammad Afandi al-Atasi, mufti of Homs, and his brother Amin Afandi, his fatwa secretary. And among them: Shaykh Ahmad Sulayman al-Arwadi and others whose mention here would be lengthy; their number is incalculable among the excellent and notable; they benefited from him, took from him, and graduated under him. His works: The scholar Ibn Abidin began authorship at the age of seventeen, when he began commenting on many books he read under his teacher al-Aqqad, among them: his commentary on al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and others. He continued practicing learning, teaching, and authorship until he became the reference for fatwa and was entrusted with the trust of fatwa during the time of the Mufti of Damascus, the scholar Shaykh Husayn al-Muradi. Questions came to him from many places and distant regions, and he answered them with excellent research and wonderful writing. He authored many treatises in that regard. As for the Hashiya (commentary) which is the greatest of what he left, it is the pillar of the Hanafi school and its impregnable door; it comprises the essence of his thought and the best of his research. This is because he saw al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its conciseness that closed many of its issues, and he saw that a commentary was necessary to clarify its aims and explain its meanings. But he saw that many authors of great books, like Fath al-Qadir and others, wrote up to the chapter of Ijara, and then often the author or the teacher of the lesson would die, leaving the book incomplete. Therefore, he began editing his book from the chapter of Ijara, saying: "If time does not allow, this book of mine will be a completion of others' incompletions; if time allows, I will return to complete them." And indeed he began composing his Hashiya under the supervision of his teacher Shaykh Sa'id al-Halabi after having read al-Durr with him and commented on it, then read al-Durr a second time with the Hashiya of Ibrahim al-Halabi. During this time, he would present it to his teacher from time to time, and he would admire it, declaring: "Is it not time for this pile to end?" He started from the chapter of Ijara to the end, as mentioned, then returned to the beginning and completed up to the chapter of Ijara again until the whole book was complete. Then he began to make a fair copy, as he had started, but time did not permit him to complete the fair copy of all, until Allah arranged for his son Ala al-Din to complete the fair copy of what his father had begun from his manuscript, and they were added in two independent volumes which he named Qurrat 'Uyun al-Akhyar bi-Takmilat Radd al-Muhtar. He mentioned his father's biography at the beginning of the Takmila and mentioned his conventions; refer to it.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a biographical introduction (tarjama) from the investigator's preface, detailing the life, teachers, students, and works of the great Hanafi jurist Ibn Abidin. It lists many of his students who became scholars and judges, showing his widespread influence. The passage then focuses on his magnum opus, the Hashiya known as Radd al-Muhtar 'ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Ibn Abidin began his work at age seventeen and later became the chief mufti of Damascus. He conceived his Hashiya as a completion of incomplete works like Fath al-Qadir. He started from the chapter of Ijara (leases) because many previous authors had reached that point and then died. Under the supervision of his teacher Shaykh Sa'id al-Halabi, he wrote his commentary. Ultimately, he completed the work, and after his death, his son Ala al-Din finished the fair copy and published it. This Hashiya is considered the most relied-upon reference in the Hanafi school. The passage includes a quote from Ibn Abidin explaining his method and the famous remark of his teacher: 'Has the time not come for this pile (of work) to end?'

Key Terms
ابن عابدين (Ibn Abidin): The author of Radd al-Muhtar, a leading Hanafi jurist of the 19th century, whose full name is Muhammad Amin ibn Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz al-Shami.
الحاشية (al-Hashiya): The supercommentary or gloss; here referring specifically to Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar, which is a commentary on al-Durr al-Mukhtar by Haskafi.
الدر المختار (al-Durr al-Mukhtar): A concise manual of Hanafi fiqh by Haskafi, which is the base text for Ibn Abidin's Hashiya.
أمانة الفتوى (Amanat al-Fatwa): The trust of issuing fatwas; the position of official mufti entrusted with giving authoritative legal opinions.
قرة عيون الأخيار بتكملة رد المحتار (Qurrat 'Uyun al-Akhyar bi-Takmilat Radd al-Muhtar): The title of the two-volume supplement written by Ibn Abidin's son, Ala al-Din, completing the fair copy of Radd al-Muhtar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
From which chapter did Ibn Abidin begin writing his Hashiya (Radd al-Muhtar), and why?
A) From the beginning (Kitab al-Tahara) because it was the natural starting point.
B) From the chapter of Ijara (leases) because many earlier scholars had stopped there and their works remained incomplete.
C) From the chapter of Nikah (marriage) because of its importance in daily life.
D) From the chapter of Jihad because he was inspired by current events.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar is the definitive Hanafi fatwa reference, written to complete earlier works and later finished by his son.

Life of the Author
beginner
8 min
Ibn Abidin: The Author of Radd al-Muhtar and His Scholarly Legacy
Biographical account of Ibn Abidin's scholarly life and works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key biographical facts about Ibn Abidin, explain his motivation for writing Radd al-Muhtar, and describe the significance of his work in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar is the final authority of the Hanafi school—a commentary born from the need to complete the unfinished works of earlier scholars.

This lesson introduces you to the life and works of the great Hanafi jurist Ibn Abidin (d. 1252 AH). You will see how his scholarly journey led him to become the mufti of Damascus and how his magnum opus, Radd al-Muhtar, was composed to fill gaps left by earlier commentators. Understanding his method and motivation is essential for anyone who intends to rely on his work for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Author and His Role
Read the initial lines to learn who Ibn Abidin was, under whom he studied, and his eventual position as mufti of Damascus.
He was entrusted with the responsibility of fatwa by the mufti of Damascus, Sheikh Husayn al-Muradi.
2
Trace His Scholarly Motivation
Understand why he decided to write Radd al-Muhtar. He noticed that many earlier scholars had written commentaries only up to the chapter of Ijarah and then died, leaving the work incomplete.
He started his commentary from the chapter of Ijarah to fill those gaps, planning to complete the beginning if time allowed.
3
Understand the Completion Process
Note that he lived to finish the entire work but died before completing the fair copy. His son, Ala al-Din, completed the fair copy and added two volumes of supplements.
The supplement is called Qurrat Uyun al-Akhyar bi-Takmilat Radd al-Muhtar.
4
Recognize the Symbols Used
Learn the symbols 'T' (for al-Tahtawi) and 'H' (for al-Halabi) used in the marginal notes of Radd al-Muhtar.
When he adds 'taammul' or 'fihi nazar' after quoting others, it indicates subtle criticism.
5
List His Major Works
Identify his two most important works: Radd al-Muhtar (the mainstay of the Hanafi school) and Minhat al-Khaliq (glosses on al-Bahr al-Raiq).
The passage also mentions the various editions of Radd al-Muhtar.

ثم عاد إلى البداية، وأتم ذلك إلى باب الإجارة أيضاً حتى تم الكتاب كاملا ثم شرع بالتبييض، كما بدأ، ولم يسمح له الأجل بتبييض الجميع حتى قيض الله تعالى ولده علاء الدين، فأكمل تبييض ما بدأ به والده عن خطه، وألحقت بمجلدين مستقلين سماها قرة عيون الأخيار بتكملة رد المحتار وقد ذكر ترجمة والده في بداية التكملة، وذكر اصطلاحاته، فارجع إليها . لم يكن للعلامة ابن عابدين كثير من الرموز والاصطلاحات المغمضة في كتابه الحاشية، فحيث رمز ط فمراده العلامة الطحطاوي في حاشيته على الدر ، وحيث رمز ح فمراده العلامة الحلبي، محشى الدر المختار . وفي الجزئين الرابع والخامس بعض الرموز التي ينقلها عن بعض الكتب كـ القنية وغيرها تعرف بمكانها، وهو في حاشيته كعادته في جميع كتبه على أدب جم مع العلماء السابقين . إلا أنه غالبًا ما يورد عبارة الطحطاوى أو الحلبي أو غيرهما، ويضيف بعدها تأمل أو فليحرر أو فيه نظر أو غير ذلك، وحينئذ مراده أن لا يُسلّم بذلك بل يردّه بشكل غير ظاهر تأدبا . فهرس مؤلفاته : ترك العلامة المترجم كثيراً من المصنفات القيمة، والعديد من الرسائل النافعة التي تشهد بعلو قدره وباهر تحقيقه وهي ۱ - Introduction to the critical edition: Biography of the Author. He began writing commentaries on many of the books he studied under his teacher al-ʿAqqād, among them his commentary on al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq and al-Durr al-Mukhtār and others. He continued practicing knowledge, learning, teaching, and authorship until he became the reference for fatwa and was entrusted with the responsibility of fatwa during the era of the Mufti of Damascus, the scholar Sheikh Ḥusayn al-Murādī. Questions came to him from many places and distant regions, and he answered them with splendid research and exquisite composition. He authored many treatises on that. As for the Ḥāshiya, which is the greatest thing he left, it is the mainstay of the Ḥanafī school and its fortified gate; he included in it the essence of his thought and the cream of his research. That is because he saw al-Durr al-Mukhtār and the conciseness in it that closed many of its issues, and he saw that a commentary was necessary to clarify its aims and explain its meanings. But he saw that many authors of major books like Fatḥ al-Qadīr and others had written up to the chapter of Ijāra, and then often the author or teacher of the lesson died, leaving the book incomplete. Therefore he began editing this book of his from the chapter of Ijāra, saying: 'If life does not help, this book of mine will be a completion of the deficiencies of others; if life helps, I will return to complete them.' Indeed, he began composing this Ḥāshiya under the supervision of his teacher Sheikh Saʿīd al-Ḥalabī after reading al-Durr with him and commenting on it. Then he read al-Durr again to him with the Ḥāshiya of Ibrāhīm al-Ḥalabī. During that, he would present it to his teacher from time to time, who admired it, declaring that, saying: 'Is it not time for this pile to end?' He began first from the chapter of Ijāra to its end as mentioned, then he returned to the beginning and completed it up to the chapter of Ijāra as well until the entire book was complete. Then he began writing it in fair copy as he started, but life did not permit him to complete the fair copy of all of it until Allāh the Exalted brought forth his son ʿAlāʾ al-Dīn, who completed the fair copy of what his father had begun from his handwriting, and it was appended in two independent volumes which he called Qurrat ʿUyūn al-Akhyār bi-Takmilat Radd al-Muhtār. He mentioned his father's biography at the beginning of the supplement and mentioned his conventions; refer to it. Al-ʿAllāma Ibn ʿĀbidīn did not have many obscure symbols and conventions in his book al-Ḥāshiya. Where he uses 'Ṭ', he means al-ʿAllāma al-Ṭaḥṭāwī in his Ḥāshiya on al-Durr; where he uses 'Ḥ', he means al-ʿAllāma al-Ḥalabī, the annotator of al-Durr al-Mukhtār. In the fourth and fifth parts there are some symbols that he transmits from some books like al-Qunyah and others, which are known in their places. He, in his Ḥāshiya, as is his habit in all his books, observes excellent etiquette with earlier scholars. Except that he often quotes the expression of al-Ṭaḥṭāwī or al-Ḥalabī or others, and then adds 'taʾammal' or 'fal-yuḥarrar' or 'fīhi naẓar' or such, and by that he means that one should not accept it without question, but rather refute it subtly out of courtesy. Index of his works: The author left many valuable compositions and numerous useful treatises that testify to his high rank and brilliant research. They are: 1 - al-Ḥāshiya Radd al-Muhtār ʿalā al-Durr al-Mukhtār: This is the mainstay of the Ḥanafī school, and the greatest of his widely circulated books. It has been published in many editions, the finest being: the Būlāq edition in 1272 AH in five volumes, then in 1276, then 1299, then in the Maymaniyya Press 1307, likewise in Istanbul 1307, then in Maymaniyya again 1323, then in the Bābī al-Ḥalabī Press in eight volumes with the supplement, then in Istanbul, and has been reproduced many times. 2 - Minḥat al-Khāliq: These are glosses that he first wrote in the margins of its pages, then collected them. In them, he mostly only addresses what requires clarification or strengthening, or what involves discussion or difficulty, except in some places that need editing from al-Nahr al-Fāʾiq by ʿUmar ibn Nujaym, and some of what was written by Sheikh Khayr al-Dīn al-Ramlī. He named it Minḥat al-Khāliq ʿalā al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq. And indeed, Ibn ʿĀbidīn completed the commentary of Ibn Nujaym which reached W.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a biography of the author of Radd al-Muhtar, Ibn Abidin, taken from the introduction to the critical edition. It describes his career as a scholar, his teaching under his teacher al-Aqqad, and his specialization in fatwa. The highlight is his magnum opus, the Hāshiya known as Radd al-Muhtar, which he wrote as a commentary on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. The passage explains his motivation: he saw that many scholars had written commentaries up to the chapter of Ijarah but died before completing them, so he started his work from that chapter, intending to fill the gaps if his life was short, or to complete from the beginning if he lived long. He did live to complete the work, but died before finishing the fair copy; his son Ala al-Din completed it. The text also details his symbols: 'T' for al-Tahtawi and 'H' for al-Halabi. It lists his major works, with Radd al-Muhtar as the foundational text of the Hanafi school, and Minhat al-Khaliq as a gloss on al-Bahr al-Raiq. The passage reflects the typical scholarly concern for accuracy, etiquette, and completion of works.

Key Terms
الحاشية رد المحتار (al-Ḥāshiya Radd al-Muhtār): Ibn Abidin's commentary on al-Durr al-Mukhtar, considered the primary reference of the Hanafi school.
منحة الخالق (Minḥat al-Khāliq): A collection of glosses by Ibn Abidin on al-Bahr al-Raiq of Ibn Nujaym and others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar is the foundational text for Hanafi fatwa, written to complete the work of earlier scholars and distinguished by its comprehensive research and scholarly etiquette.

Ibn Abidin's Works: Foundations of Hanafi Fatwa
List of Ibn Abidin's major writings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the most authoritative Hanafi fatwa work and list at least five key writings of Ibn Abidin.

Core Idea
Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar is the bedrock of Hanafi fatwa, and his bibliography shows the breadth of his mastery.

This lesson introduces the scholarly legacy of Ibn Abidin, the paramount late Hanafi jurist. His major works, especially Radd al-Muhtar, are essential references for fatwa. By studying his bibliography, you'll appreciate the depth of Hanafi scholarship and the sources a mufti relies upon.

Guided Path
1
Recognize Ibn Abidin's status
Ibn Abidin (d. 1252 AH) is the most relied-upon Hanafi jurist of the later period. His works are primary references for fatwa in the school.
He lived in Damascus and authored over 17 works.
2
Identify the primary work
The book 'Radd al-Muhtar ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar' (also called 'al-Hashiyah') is described as the foundation (umdah) of the Hanafi school.
It is a supercommentary on al-Durr al-Mukhtar by al-Haskafi.
3
Explore other significant works
Other notable works include 'Minhat al-Khaliq' (a gloss on al-Bahr al-Ra'iq), 'al-Uqud al-Durriyyah' (revision of fatwas), and many glosses on usul, tafsir, and Arabic.
These works cover fiqh, usul, tafsir, balaghah, and more.
4
Note the printing history
Radd al-Muhtar has been printed multiple times, with the Bulaq edition (1272 AH) being the best. This history shows its continuous use.
Later editions include supplements and corrections.
